  229. I can't say it any better than boostjuice already has, the Oral-B Pro-Health Battery line are a great series of alternative electric toothbrushes.<br /><br />Compared to the previous line of Vitality brushes (which had a simple induction recharger base), the Pro-Health Battery line uses two AA's (not AAA's as stated in their description) to power the brush. The gives it a heavier weight, although you do get a more consistent motor strength. The motor won't slow down when battery power gets too low, it will just stop.<br /><br />Speaking of the Pro-Health motor, it's pretty powerful. It shook my hand as much as Oral-B's pulsar brush line and is about as powerful as a video-game controller vibrating motor. This was a significantly more than the Vitality line.<br /><br />The Pro-Health does have a neat water-proof battery compartment, and a little petrolium jelly will not only water-proof it further, it will make opening the thing easier when there are no batteries inside. With batteries inside, pressing the two tabs at the base will cause the door to spring open.<br /><br />The bottom-line: the Oral-B Pro-Health Battery line is a powerful, affordable toothbrush. It might be twice as heavy and twice as shakey as the Vitality line (whose brush heads it is still compatible with), but it's also half the price and offers the consumer the convenience of swapping out batteries.

  301. UPDATE: I note that several reviewers complain about the fit of the two AA batteries inside the battry compartment. Another viewer said to clean the goop off of the battery cap and around the sealing rubber O-ring that keeps the batteries dry. He's correct. About every week, I take off the cap, carefully roll the rubber O-ring off the handle, and clean the goop (toothpaste; dried saliva; etc) from the groove where the rubber O-ring rested, and the cap and batteries go back without issue. There IS, repeat IS, a spring in the battery cap to compensate for longer or shorter batteries. In the long run, rechargeable Nickel Metal Hydride batteries are FAR cheaper than Alkaline batteries, and they sure beat having some plug-in toothbrush charger cluttering up your bathroom sink. ---ORIGINAL POST:----> One dental technician complimented me on the care of my teeth. I told her I was using the Oral-B Pro Health. She spoke highly of the other major brand of \\"supersonic\\" rechargeable toothbrushes, so I did a bit of research. First, 99% of the effectiveness of any toothbrush, manual or electric, depends on the technique used by the mouth owner. Second, I found something from the ADA or some other professional organization that reported \\"there's not a dime's worth of difference in the effectiveness of various brands of electronic toothbrushes.\\" It went on to say that user preference is the discriminator, NOT whether one scours off more plaque than the other. Third, I learned that the \\"supersonic\\" toothbrushes move their heads up and down across your tooth, while this Oral-B rotates its head a bizillion times a second. I can easily move it over and down into the gumline, then massage the entire gum for as long as I wish. Fourth, take a look at the cost of the replacement heads of this toothbrush (not the knockoffs, but the branded packages) and you'll see a big difference from the \\"supersonic\\" toothbrushes. Fifth, I've been recharging AA alkalaine batteries for a few years and using them in this toothbrush, but now I've moved to NiMH (nickel metal hydride) to see if it makes any difference. Like other raters said, you can keep recharging these batteries -- up to 250 times according to the manufacturer -- for as long as the toothbrush motor lasts. So far, for me, it's been four years. My wife and I both use this Oral-B, twice daily, seven days a week (different brush heads of course). Other reviewers have pointed out that when the other built-in \\"rechargeable\\" batteries wear out (and they will), then you have to throw away the whole unit. Meanwhile, I'm recharging my batteries and just replacing them as needed. I don't want any plug-in electric chargers cluttering my bathroom sink top, and I don't want to hide them in the medicine cabinet and drag it it from time to time to recharge my \\"built in\\" rechargeable \\"supersonic\\" toothbrush. Replaceable batteries are definitely the best choice -- for me. One last item, I could care less about five different \\"modes,\\" timers, neon lights and other bells and whistles. I know to brush in each quadrant of my mouth for 30-40 seconds each (more than a two-minute total); I know to massage my gums when finished; I know to brush my tounge and roof of the mouth afterwards. I don't need to spend another $100 on a simple toothbrush to remind me to do these things. This model of the Oral-B costs approximately ONE TENTH as much as the top of the line \\"supersonic\\" toothbrush. Ladies and gentlemen of the jury, I leave the decision to you.for a few years and using them in this toothbrush, but now I've moved to NiMH (nickel metal hydride) to see if it makes any difference.   433. If your sick and tired of buying rechargeable toothbrushes with internal batteries that quickly loose their charge capacity and can't be replaced, then this toothbrush is for you. These toothbrushes also feature electronics that keep the motor voltage constant - and hence maintain optimal orbital brushing speed - even as the battery voltage declines as they discharge. So motor power is held consistant right up until the end when the batteries near complete depletion where finally the motor just stops. So you get optimal performance right to the end of set of batteries! This feature also means despite the lower voltage of using 2x 1.2V NiMH rechargeable cells, you get the same brushing speed and power as using 2x 1.5V Alkaline cells. This is great! as rechargeable NiMH cells last longer due to holding more charge capacity, are cheaper in the long-term, and save you throwing out bucket loads of spent Alkaline cells that would end up in landfill.<br /><br />Build quality of the units is great as you'd expect for a Braun, even for a cheaper unit. The battery chamber seals well but can be perfected by applying a thin layer of vaseline or other grease around the O-ring of the lower snap out section. This guarantees moisture vapour can't get into the battery chamber which over time can corrode rechargable batteries in long term service.<br /><br />The on/off switch is smartly sealed beneath a rubber membrane which ensures water can't get into the internals.<br /><br />The 2-minute timer is a nice feature. At this time interval the motor briefly oscillates off-on-off-on to let you know how long you've been brushing but if you wan't to keep brushing, you can as it doesnt turn the motor off.<br /><br />Overall this brush is great, especially for the price. The fact that ageing rechargable batteries can be replaced as their capacity diminishes means you can expect years of service from one of these, whereas the more expensive units with internal batteries are useless junk as soon as they loose the majority of their capacity.<br /><br />I changed from using a Spinbrush Pro clean to this Braun, and the improvement is terrific. The Spinbrush ran too-slow on lower voltage rechargable batteries, the battery chamber leaked, the button corroded and became unreliable, and batteries quickly corroded due to the leaking chamber. All these problems are remedied with this Braun model.capacity. I changed from using a Spinbrush Pro clean to this Braun, and the improvement is terrific. However be careful for this workaround - make sure you don't short circuit.<br /><br />UPDATE on May 20, 2013<br />The battery cap broke on May 20, 2013. I started used it in Jan 2011, so it life-cycle is about a little over 2 years.

  709. I've had this toothbrush since the start of 2011, and it still works like new. I'm really surprised by how good it is, especially considering its price. It does a great job cleaning and performs consistently even when the batteries are low.<br /><br />This device is compatible with the usual replacement toothbrush heads, and it works with standard AA batteries. The high-end models have integrated batteries, so when they eventually wear out, you have to replace the whole device. In contrast, using this model with a pair of good rechargeable batteries will go a long way. Batteries such as the Sanyo Eneloop can be recharged over 1000 times, and if they wear out, you can simply get a new pair of batteries, rather than purchase another toothbrush. Fully charged batteries can easily get you three or four weeks of use, so you would have to use the toothbrush for more than 50 years before the batteries wore out, anyway. I doubt any electric toothbrush would last that long, but the point is that the battery is not the limiting factor in the lifespan of this particular model.<br /><br />I don't particularly care for the extra features on the really expensive models (electronic displays, etc.). They seem pointless to me, and I suspect having numerous extraneous features increases the probability that the toothbrush will break. The pricey models cost 5 to 10 times as much as this basic one, but you can bet they won't last 5 to 10 times as long. I spent around $100 on an electric toothbrush a few years ago, and it didn't last more than a year. This $11 version is still going after at least two years.<br /><br />There's absolutely no reason to spend more than $50 on an electric toothbrush. Expensive models don't last long enough to justify the price, and they don't clean any better than the simpler ones. If you just want the benefit of oscillation-rotation cleaning and not a bunch of fancy but useless features, get this toothbrush. You won't be disappointed.

  711. First thing to mention here: They fixed the battery compartment!!!<br /><br />Unfortunately , there are no instructions on how to replace the batteries; in fact, no instructions at all, though it's fairly intuitive. The next paragraph explains how to do this on the current version of this model. It is very easy for me, and I don't have a lot of strength in my fingers!!!<br /><br />I didn't have an earlier version of this toothbrush, but apparently it was a nightmare to attempt to replace the batteries on this model. It is a piece of cake now, but there are no directions to explain how to do it. If you just try and pull the bottom cover off, you will probably not succeed; in fact, you might break the cap so that it will not hold the batteries in place. Here's what you need to do: Press firmly on both raised dots in the center of the blue half-circles just above the bottom cover. It will release the cap. Then gently pull the cap away from the bottom . The polarity of the batteries is marked on each side of the bottom of the brush. It is hidden by the cover but you can see it when the cover is removed. The cap will go on the base in either direction.<br /><br />Alternatively, you can press one raised dot on one side, then the other, and then remove the cap. It can't get much easier than this. I feel for those who bought earlier versions of this toothbrush, but the good news is that the company listened to the complaints and made changes. I bought this one knowing I could get a refund if it was difficult to work with the battery compartment, and I was quite happy that it was a breeze to change batteries. Since the batteries come packed separately and are not installed inside the device, I had to remove the cap to insert the Duracell brand AA batteries before I could use it, so I got to test that part out right away. I did this several times to try and see what all the complaints were about regarding the bottom battery compartment cover, but I didn't see anything to gripe about. I believe that those who complained must have had an earlier version of this one. I know it's not exactly the same device because they advertise on the back of the package \\"Improved technology delivers more consistent battery performance\\" which indicates that they made at least one change to it. I do not know how long the batteries last yet since I just got this today and made some comparisons to the rechargeable model I have. If you have any problems inserting the batteries on this newer version of this model, don't force the cover on; just try using the same brand (Duracell) that came with it and you should have no problems.<br /><br />This electric toothbrush works very well. No gimmicks, it just works. It's the battery-powered version of the one recommended to me by my dental hygienist (which was the Oral B Professional Healthy Clean Precision 1000). It's very similar in most respects except for a few things. It has removable batteries, not rechargeable fixed batteries inside the case. The ones that come with it are Duracell AA's, but you might be able to use rechargeable NiMH batteries, which I have not tried (yet).<br /><br />It has a 2-minute timer for brushing your teeth; after 2 minutes have elapsed, it shuts itself off. (Of course, you can turn it back on again if you need to.) Unlike the rechargeable version I mentioned above, it does not vibrate differently every 30 seconds to remind you to move on to another quarter of your mouth. (I would have paid a bit more for that feature.) There are no instructions to explain about the timer, but it has the word \\"timer\\" just below the ON button (which doesn't have the word \\"ON\\" anywhere near it) and it mentions it on the package as well. I can see someone trying this out for the first time and thinking that it was defective. Braun would do well to include a slip of paper with brief instructions on this device. It would make using the brush a much better experience for first-time users.<br /><br />This uses the same brush heads that the the Oral B Professional Healthy Clean Precision 1000 uses (several other rechargeable models also use the same brush heads also), so they should be readily available. There are at least three types of brush heads for the Oral B toothbrushes. I like the ones with the small round brush only, but there are dual-action types also if you prefer those, and at least one other type. I did exchange the heads between my rechargeable Oral B toothbrush and this one and they fit either handle. That being the case, this may be less expensive in the long over buying those $5 throw-away battery-powered toothbrushes that have no replaceable heads, especially if the generic replacement heads work out. It would feel wasteful to me, throwing a powered brush away every 3-6 months or so.<br /><br />There are generic brush heads available. I haven't tried any of these yet, but I have read that some generic brush heads do not last and break. Some work, others don't, and you can have a mix of these in a batch if you buy them from online auction sites. If you buy them here you can send them back for a refund if you are not satisfied. There's some satisfaction in knowing that, but it can be an aggravation to return them, so you may just want to buy the brand name replacement brush heads.<br /><br />The brushes have some bristles with a tint that fades over time to let you know when it's time to replace it. I think it's more of a marketing tool to get you to buy replacement brush heads more often, but you can use that as a reminder if you prefer. I will replace mine when it feels too soft.<br /><br />The brush heads have a colored ring between the head and handle so you can tell one from another if more than two people in a household use the same product. The replacement heads, if you buy them in a multi-pack, have different colored rings, so if you buy one of those packs when you buy the product, you will probably have several colors to choose from. Alternatively, you could color one ring with fingernail polish and let it dry before putting it on a brush head, or just use some colored tape around one or more of the main parts of the device in your bathroom. You could even make a label for each handle with the user's name on it. Or just put the brushes in different places for each family member.<br /><br />Overall, this is much better than the inexpensive ones I've had in the past. I will definitely brush my teeth more often now that it's so easy to do. Easy to do? How hard can it be to brush your teeth with a regular toothbrush?<br /><br />You have to try one of these to understand. I tend to brush too quickly and I'm not very thorough, and this device helps me time how long I brush in each quarter of my mouth. Sure, I could set a timer and keep an eye on it, switching to different parts of my mouth as the timer counts down, but this is very convenient. It also probably does a better job without brushing too hard.<br /><br />When I'm done using this brush or the rechargeable one I have, I feel like I just came from the dentist's office having had my teeth cleaned. That's better than any powered or manual brush I've ever used in the past. I can highly recommend this powered toothbrush!<br /><br />[Edit: I've had this toothbrush for 2 months and I'm still on the same set of batteries that came with it. I use this daily at work, 5 days per week, to put this into perspective for you, but I was surprised that I got 2 months of use out of the original batteries. I plan to try rechargeable NiMH batteries and keep one of my small chargers at work when these batteries are finally exhausted.]<br /><br />If this review was helpful, please click the YES button below this review.
